data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D44369%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDaming%2BChen%252C%2BZhiquan%2BHuang%252C%2BShangqi%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c12470%26title%3DA%2BFlexible%2BMulti-Channel%2BHollow%2BCNT%252FCarbon%2BNanofiber%2BComposites%2Bwith%2BS%252FN%2BCo-Doping%2Bfor%2BSodium%252FPotassium%2BIon%2BEnergy%2BStorage%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D44378%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2021" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Carbon fibrous materials are the promising candidate for the anode of flexible sodium-ion batteries and potassium-ion batteries due to the structural advantages. However, the progress of mechanically robust anode materials with high electrochemical properties is still unsatisfactory for the flexible electrodes. Herein, the comprehensive design of the morphology with unique multi-channel hollow 1D/1D carbon nanotube/carbon nanofiber network and the lattice structure of carbon with S/N co-doping has been proposed. Benefiting from the enlarged interlayer spacing and the flexible fibrous network, the S/N doped carbon nanotube/carbon nanofiber composites (CNT/SNCF) possess not only high conductivity but also good structural stability during sodiation and potassiation processes. When used as anode materials in SIBs and PIBs, the free-standing CNT/SNCF electrodes exhibit high discharge capacities (274.1 and 212.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A/g after 1000 cycles, respectively), superior cycle stability (150.4 and 100.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A/g after 5000 cycles, respectively) and rate performance (109.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A/g and 108.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A/g, respectively), showing great prospects in flexible energy storage devices.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D44369%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDaming%2BChen%252C%2BZhiquan%2BHuang%252C%2BShangqi%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c12470%26title%3DA%2BFlexible%2BMulti-Channel%2BHollow%2BCNT%252FCarbon%2BNanofiber%2BComposites%2Bwith%2BS%252FN%2BCo-Doping%2Bfor%2BSodium%252FPotassium%2BIon%2BEnergy%2BStorage%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D44378%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291646&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanotubes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon%20nanotubes" class="article__tags__link">Carbon nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291009&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanofibers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanofibers" class="article__tags__link">Nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292006&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Potassium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Potassium" class="article__tags__link">Potassium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sulfur+And+Nitrogen&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sulfur%20and%20nitrogen">sulfur and nitrogen</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Flexible&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_flexible">flexible</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Multi-channel+Hollow&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_multi-channel%20hollow">multi-channel hollow</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Potassium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_potassium-ion%20batteries">potassium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Flexible energy storage devices, especially rapidly-developed flexible lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), have been integrated into our life in many applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, the scarcity of lithium push people to seek other alternatives. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Among various choices, electrochemical devices based upon naturally abundant and cheap alkali metal ions (sodium ion Na<sup>+</sup>, potassium ion K<sup>+</sup>) attract great attention. In addition, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) have suitable redox potentials <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 2 and 5 to 8">(2,5−8)</a> and similar rocking chair mechanism as LIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> giving vast designing space and commercial prospects. Notwithstanding, the large radii of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) and K<sup>+</sup> (1.38 Å), which are much greater than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), cause the poor diffusion kinetics as well as severe volume change of electrode materials during charge/discharge, thus resulting in unsatisfactory capacity and cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 6 and 10 to 12">(6,10−12)</a> Meanwhile, the flexible electrodes are required to sustain the electrochemical performance under repeated mechanical deformation, which makes further demands for the structural stability. Therefore, the development of mechanically robust electrode materials with good electrochemical properties is the critical challenge for flexible SIBs and PIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Carbon materials, which dominate the anodes in commercial LIBs due to high electron conductivity, large voltage window, low cost and excellent cycling stability, have been considered as the potential anode materials for SIBs and KIBs. However, the small lattice space (0.345 nm for graphite) is unsatisfactory to store and transport Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup>. On the other hand, as covalent bonded carbon in bulk is brittle, appropriate structural design is necessary to enhance its deformability for use. Thus, the design in both lattice structure and nano/micro-structure is required to enlarge the Na<sup>+</sup> or K<sup>+</sup> storage with rapid and steady intercalation reactions under external deformation.</div><div class="NLM_p">There are different forms of carbon materials such as 0D (<i>e.g.</i> quantum dots), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> 1D carbon materials (<i>e.g.</i> carbon nanofibers CNFs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> carbon nanotube CNT <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a>), 2D sheets (<i>e.g.</i> graphene) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> and 3D nanostructures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> Among them, 1D carbon materials are attractive for flexible electrodes due to their superior flexibility, fast electronic transport path along axial direction, and high stability against volume change caused by redox reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a> CNFs are easier to produce, especially in the form of 3D network which can be fabricated by the facile electrostatic spinning with subsequent carbonization. But the graphitization degree is usually low, thus degrading the electrical conductivity and mechanical properties. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref28" aria-label="reference 11 and 28">(11,28)</a> In contrast, CNTs have outstanding mechanical properties and conductivity. Hence, it is reasonable to achieve the optimized mechanical and electrochemical properties through compositing both CNFs and CNTs. When they are weaved together, a flexible network with fast charge transfer can be achieved. In addition, it is feasible to introduce various micro/nano-scale pores and multi-channel hollows to enlarge the active sites to boost the charge transfer and the capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref26 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref26 ref29" aria-label="reference 14, 26 and 29">(14,26,29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In term of the small layer spacing of carbon, heteroatom doping is an effective method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 23 and 30 to 33">(23,30−33)</a> For instance, the doping of sulfur with the atomic radius 0.104 nm can not only increase the layer spacing, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 2, 34 and 35">(2,34,35)</a> but also benefit the conductivity and chemical reactivity of carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> leading to improved Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> storage. The doping of nitrogen can also ameliorate the electrochemical reactivity, conductivity and surface wettability of carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> So far there are quite a few reports <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref25" aria-label="reference 15 and 25">(15,25)</a> that use heteroatom doping and hierarchical pores to increase the electrochemical properties of carbon nanofibers, especially for LIBs. However, a rational design of the morphological structure and the lattice structure for the more challenging sodium/potassium storage is still scarce.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, this work demonstrates a comprehensive design from both the morphological view using 1D/1D carbon composite structure, high porosity and multi-channel hollows, and the lattice structure view using the S/N co-doping. The developed CNT/SNCF shows not only reliable flexibility but also remarkable electrochemical performance. As the anode for SIBs, it exhibits high cycle stability (274.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A/g after 1000 cycles, 150.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A/g after 5000 cycles) and rate performance. As the anode for PIBs, it also shows excellent reversible capacity (315.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A/g after 300 cycles) and cycle stability (100.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A/g after 5000 cycles). These results indicate that the CNT/SNCF electrode material possesses exceptional sodium and potassium storage performance and has great prospects in flexible batteries including LIBs, SIBs and PIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Preparation of ZnO/Carbon Nanotubes/Polyacrylonitrile (ZnO/CNT PAN) Nanofibers</h3><div class="NLM_p last">According to the work, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> 1.0 g of PAN (MW 15,000) was dissolved into 5.0 mL of DMF and stirred for 1 h, named as solution A. Meanwhile, 0.1 g CNT and 1.2 g ZnO nanosheets were added into 5.0 mL DMF by ultrasonicating for 1 h until CNT and ZnO were uniformly dispersed in DMF. The obtain solution is termed as solution B. Solution A and B were mixed and stirred for 12 h. During the electrospinning process, the flow rate, the voltage, the time and the distance were 1.0 mL h<sup>–1</sup>, 20 kV, 5 h and 15 cm, respectively. The obtained nanofiber is named as ZnO/CNT/PAN.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Preparation of Flexible Multichannel Hollow S/N Co-Doped Carbon Nanotubes/Carbon Nanofibers (CNT/SNCF)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The ZnO/CNT/PAN nanofibers (200 mg) with sulfur powder (300 mg) were calcined sequentially at 350 °C for 2 h and then 700 °C for 2 h in Ar atmosphere with the constant rate of 2 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. The applied two-step calcination protocol is because the rapid carbonization in the direct treatment at high temperature of 700 °C is harmful to the fibrous structure. The product was then immersed in HCl (1 mol/L) for 24 h, then rinsed with excessive deionized water until it was neutral, finally dried at 90 °C for 6 h in vacuum. The obtained materials were the S/N co-doped carbon nanotubes/carbon nanofibers (CNT/SNCF). Similar steps were also carried out to obtain the N-doped carbon nanotubes/carbon nanofibers (CNT/NCF), the S/N co-doped carbon nanofibers (SNCF) and the N-doped carbon nanofibers (NCF) by removing the addition of sulfur powder, CNT, both sulfur and CNT, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology and microstructure of the samples were investigated by SEM, TEM and HRTEM. The crystallographic characteristics was analyzed by XRD and Raman test. The chemical states were measured by XPS, the specific surface area and porosity analysis were performed by nitrogen adsorption apparatus. The equipment and testing conditions are given in our previous work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The free-standing CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF, and NCF films were cut into small pieces (1.0–1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>) and then directly used as the electrodes in CR2032 cells, which were assembled in an Ar-filled glove box. The sodium or potassium foil and the glass fiber (Whatman) were used as the counter electrode and the separator, respectively. The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC: DEC (v/v 1:1) with 5% FEC or 0.8 M KPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DEC (v/v 1:1). The galvanostatic discharge–charge (GCD) processes were measured with a window of 0.01 ∼ 3.0 V. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impendence spectra (EIS) measurements <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> were also performed.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  General Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows the synthesis process of the S/N co-doped carbon nanotubes/carbon nanofibers (CNT/SNCF). Briefly, ZnO nanosheets (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), CNT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) and PAN were dissolved in DMF to get the ZnO/CNT/PAN nanofibers by electrospinning. The nanofibers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) have a diameter of about 200 nm with evenly distributed ZnO nanosheets and CNTs and form a 3D network skeleton. Through vulcanization, the PAN is gradually carbonized to form N-doped carbon, and sulfur simultaneously becomes small molecules that can dope into carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> and also react with ZnO to form ZnS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). ZnS is finally removed by etching with HCl to leave pores in CNT/SNCF. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows that the prepared material can be deformed into various forms. Furthermore, it can be directly cut into electrode sheets without breakage, indicating the good flexibility. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> shows the tensile strength of CNT/SNCF is about 22.6 KPa, indicating that it also processes certain mechanical stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> For comparison, NCF and S-doped NCF (SNCF) as well as the carbon nanotubes/carbon nanofibers without S-doping (CNT/NCF) have been also prepared according to the procedures in the schematic diagram.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration for the synthesis of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The typical SEM images show that the flexible CNT/SNCF is composed of 1D porous nanofibers with a diameter of about 200 nm in a 3D interconnected architecture (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a <b>and</b><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a><b>)</b>, which are similar to the original ZnO/CNT/PAN in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a &amp;b</a>, indicating that the HCl etching has negligible effects on the shape and size of the nanofibers. The samples CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF also show the similar morphology as CNT/SNCF <b>(</b><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b-d</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) SEM of CNT/SNCF; (b-f) TEM and HRTEM of CNT/SNCF at different magnifications; (g) TEM mapping of the CNT/SNCF.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As shown in TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b<b>&amp;c,</b><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7e-h and Figure S8a</a>), CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF all present porous structures, which enable the efficient transport of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>. It is worth mentioning that CNT is well distributed and interspersed inside the CNF framework, forming a 1D/1D compositing multi-channel hollow, which can effectively improve the ion transport and the conductivity. In addition, the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images show that the interlayer spacing of SNCF (0.42 nm) and CNT (0.38 nm) in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d<b>&amp;f</b> after vulcanization are significantly larger than that of NCF (0.37 nm) and CNT (0.35 nm) in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b</a>. This shows that the doped sulfur increases the interlayer spacing of CNF and CNT, benefiting to the insertion of large Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>. The TEM elemental mappings in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g confirm that carbon, nitrogen, oxygen and sulfur elements are evenly distributed in CNT/SNCF.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the XRD patterns of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF. There is a high and broad diffraction peak at ∼25° and a weak one at ∼43°, which can be indexed to (002) plane and (100) plane, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> To further study the broad (002) peaks, the peak position and FWHM are listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. It can be seen that there is no significant change considering their broad shape. The broad peak indicates that the graphitization degree is relatively low. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> In turn, the similar peaks of various carbon materials are probably caused by the same calcination temperature of 700 °C, which is critical for the graphitization. Raman results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b show two typical carbon peaks at 1346.6 cm<sup>–1</sup> and 1584.7 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to sp<sup>3</sup>-type disordered carbon (D band) and sp<sup>2</sup>-type graphitized carbon (G band), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Notably, the intensity ratio of two bands (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>) for CNT/SNCF is 1.21, higher than that of CNT/NCF (1.15), SNCF (1.19) and NCF (1.14), mainly due to the introduction of sulfur atoms, which increase the defects in the carbon material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref31 ref34 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref31 ref34 ref43" aria-label="reference 12, 31, 34 and 43">(12,31,34,43)</a> The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm measurements are used to characterize the porous structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). It is found that the specific surface area of S-doped materials, CNT/SNCF (324.19 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and SNCF (245.26 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), are lower than the undoped ones, CNT/NCF (400.96 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and NCF (294.19 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). Although the specific surface area is slightly reduced after vulcanization, the porous structure is mainly reserved. At the same time, it is noticed that the introduction of CNT is beneficial to increase the specific surface area. According to the nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherm curves (typical type IV) in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, all the samples have the mesoporous structure.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of the CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF; (b) Raman spectra of the CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF; (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm curves and pore size distribution of the CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF; (d) XPS spectra of the CNT/SNCF and CNT/NCF, respectively (inset: chemical compositions of CNT/SNCF and CNT/NCF); (e) N 1 s, and (f) S 2p.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">XPS is used to further investigate the chemical bond configuration and the element contents of C, N, S and O in the samples of CNT/SNCF and CNT/NCF. The survey spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) show that CNT/NCF only has three peaks of C, N and O. After vulcanization, two peaks of S 2p and S 2 s can be detected in CNT/SNCF, and the surface chemical composition of S content is 5.92 at.%, indicating that S is successfully doped. In order to explore the chemical valence of the elements in CNT/SNCF, the high-resolution peaks of C, N, O and S are analyzed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e<b>&amp;f</b> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>. The high-resolution C1s spectrum in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a> can be divided into C-C/C=C, C-N, C-S, C=O and O-C=O at 284.6, 285.4, 286.5, 287.7 and 290.5 eV, respectively, which further proves that N and S have been doped. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref14 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref14 ref43" aria-label="reference 12, 14 and 43">(12,14,43)</a> The O 1 s spectrum of CNT/SNCF in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a> can be divided into three peaks at about 531.1, 532.6 and 534.6 eV, corresponding to C=O, S-O and O=C-O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The N 1 s spectrum of CNT/SNCF (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) and CNT/NCF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c</a>) can be fitted to four peaks at 298.4, 400.0, 401.1 and 404.0 eV, assigning to pyridinic-N, pyrrolic-N, graphitic-N and oxidized-N, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The S 2p spectrum of CNT/SNCF in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f includes the peak at 161.9 eV for the S=C bond, the peak at 168.1 eV for the oxidized-S ground corresponding to -C-SO<sub>x</sub>-C- bond, and two strong peaks at 163.7 and 164.9 eV, consistent with the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks for the -C-S-C- covalent bond, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 41, 45 and 46">(41,45,46)</a> It is worth mentioning that after doped with sulfur atoms, the content of pyridine N increases from 38.5% of CNT/NCF to 48.4% of CNT/SNCF, mainly because the calcination of PAN and sulfur at high temperature can result in an abatement of pyrrole N in the product (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> According to the existing literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> a higher proportion of pyridine-N in carbon materials can increase the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> and electrons, which is beneficial to improve the sodium/potassium storage performance.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">(1) Sodium storage behavior.</div><div class="NLM_p">The sodium storage behavior was evaluated by CR2032 coin cells. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the CV curves of CNT/SNCF and CNT/NCF sodium-ion half-cells at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> with a voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V. In the first cycle, the irreversible cathodic peaks are observed at ∼0.41 V for the CNT/NCF electrode and ∼ 0.82 V for the CNT/SNCF electrode, which are mainly caused by the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref50" aria-label="reference 20 and 50">(20,50)</a> In the subsequent cycles, the reduction peak at 1.6 V is ascribed to reaction between S and Na<sup>+</sup> to form Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, and the peak at 1.12 V is due to the reduction of Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a> show the GCD patterns of the CNT/SNCF electrode. It can be seen that the first discharge and charge capacities of the CNT/SNCF electrode are 754.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 370.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> respectively, and the corresponding initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) is 49.1%. The low ICE of CNT/SNCF electrode is due to the high specific surface area and well-developed pore structure, which can consume a large amount of sodium and electrolyte to form the SEI film. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b</a>, the ICE of CNT/SNCF electrode is the highest compared to the rest samples, CNT/NCF (30.5%), SNCF (45.4%) and NCF (33.3%), indicating that the S/N co-doping and CNTs can reduce the irreversible reaction. In the charging curve, the obvious platform can be observed at ∼1.86 V, which indicates that part of the CNT/SNCF capacity is contributed by the Faraday reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and S. After 300 cycles, it maintains a reversible capacity of 332.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which means a retention rate of 98.6% in the 10th cycle, further proving the good stability of the CNT/SNCF electrode. The cycling performance of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF at 0.2 A/g is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. After 300 cycles, the CNT/SNCF electrode also provides the highest reversible capacity of 333.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and stable reversible capacity with a Coulombic Efficiency (CE) close to 100%. The rate performance was measured at different current densities from 0.1 to 10 A/g (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Compared with NCT/NCF, SNCF and NCF, the CNT/SNCF electrode delivers the highest average discharge capacity of 395.5, 346.1, 295.4, 249.5, 200.5, 171.3, 152.8 and 109.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 5 and 10 A/g. When the current density returns 0.1 A/g, its discharge capacity increases to 396.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, showing high rate performance.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Charge–discharge curves of CNT/SNCF at 0.2 A/g at different cycles; (b) Rate performances of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF; (c) Cycling performances of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF at 0.2 A/g; (d) Cycling performances of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF at 1 A/g; (e) Long-term cycling performance of CNT/SNCF at 5 A/g.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To clarify the cycle stability of CNT/SNCF electrode at high current density, long cycle performance was tested at 1 A/g in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. After 1000 cycles, the capacity can still be kept at 274.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after 5000 cycles at 5 A/g, its specific capacity is still as high as 150.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. Compared with other relevant works in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>, the CNT/SNCF electrode exhibits superior cycle stability and rate performance. From the SEM and TEM analysis in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13a &amp;c</a>, the structure remains intact after 1000 cycles at a high current density of 5 A/g, which fully proves that CNT/SNCF has excellent structural stability. Such excellent performance can be attributed to the synergistic effects of the 1D/1D compositing structure and the expanded graphite interlayer spacing by S/N co-doping.</div><div class="NLM_p">(2) Potassium storage behavior.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to SIBs, the potassium storage behavior has also been studied with the voltage plateau of 0.01–3 V in the half-cell. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a> shows the CV curves of the initial three cycles at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for CNT/SNCF and CNT/NCF. Both samples show the irreversible reactions at 0.1 ∼ 0.8 V in the first scan curve due to the formed SEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref49" aria-label="reference 12 and 49">(12,49)</a> Different from the CNT/NCF, the subsequent curves of the CNT/SNCF show a cathodic peak at 0.69 V and an anodic peak at 1.8 V, which can be attributed to the interaction of K<sup>+</sup> and N dopants, the reaction of K<sup>+</sup> and S, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref49" aria-label="reference 43 and 49">(43,49)</a> The GCD curves of the CNT/SNCF electrode for the initial three cycles at 0.1 A/g are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>. It can be seen that the ICE is only 41.6%, agreeing with the CV curves. However, the ICE has been significantly improved in comparison with recently reported PIBs carbon-based anodes in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the GCD curves of the CNT/SNCF electrode from 10th to 300th cycles at 0.1 A/g. The sloping curve shows that K is adsorbed on the defective carbon surface with a high heteroatom content. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> After 100 cycles, the CNT/SNCF electrode shows stable reversible capacity as high as 355.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Remarkably, the electrode maintains high specific capacity and CE (315.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 99.4%) after 300 cycles, indicating the excellent cycling stability in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. For the rate performance in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the CNT/SNCF electrode exhibits the highest reversible capacities of 314.2, 282.9, 230.1, 190.6, 162.8, 135.4 and 108.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3 and 5 A/g. With the reverse of the current density to 0.1 A/g, the capacity is almost fully recovered (307.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), showing good tolerance.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Charge–discharge curves of CNT/SNCF at 0.1 A/g; (b) Cycling performances of CNT/SNCF at 0.1 A/g; (c) Rate performances of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF; (d) Cycling performances of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF at 1 A/g; (e) Long-term cycling performance of CNT/SNCF at 5 A/g.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performance of all the samples at high current density of 1 A/g is compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. Even at an ultrahigh current density of 5 A/g in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, a high capacity of 100.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is maintained after 5000 cycles, showing outstanding stability at high rates. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13b &amp;d and Figure S16</a><b>,</b> the cycled CNT/SNCF electrode after 1000 cycles at 5 A/g show that there is almost no damage to the nanofibers, explaining the origins of the excellent stability. In addition, the electrochemical behavior of the CNT/SNCF electrode is good compared with the recent reports in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">(3) Sodium/Potassium storage mechanism.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms of the CNT/SNCF, the electrode was scanned from 0.2 to 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a. The electrochemical storage process can be analyzed by the equation <i>i</i> = a<i>v<sup>b</sup></i> where a and b are adjustable parameters, and <i>v</i> is the sweep rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> For the anodic and cathodic processes, the value of b is calculated to be 0.87 and 0.89 respectively in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17a</a>, indicating that CNT/SNCF processes both pseudo-capacitance and insertion/extraction mechanisms. By fitting the CV curve at a specific scan rate in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b, the pseudo-capacitance contribution can be calculated. It can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c that when the scanning rate increases, the capacitive contribution becomes higher, up to 82.4% at 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Similar to SIBs, the potassium ion half-cell tests also show the similar charging mechanisms in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17b</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d-f.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. SIBs results (a) CV curves of CNT/SNCF at different scan rates; (b) CV curve with capacitive- and diffusion-controlled contributions at 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (c) Ratio of capacitive contribution in CNT/SNCF at different scan rates; PIBs results (d) CV curves of CNT/SNCF at different scan rates; (e) CV curve with capacitive- and diffusion-controlled contributions at 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (f) Ratio of capacitive contribution in CNT/SNCF at different scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a> shows the EIS curves of CNT/SNCF, CNT/NCF, SNCF and NCF at the initial state in SIBs and PIBs. All the samples possess a semicircle in the high frequency range, which represents the resistance of SEI film (R<sub>f</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) using the equivalent circuit in the inset. The low frequency range exhibits a slope, representing the solid diffusion of ions in the electrode material. The EIS results clearly show that the CNT/SNCF has the lowest R<sub>ct</sub>, indicating the kinetically favorable charge/discharge process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> The reduced R<sub>ct</sub> can be attributed to the introduction of S/N co-doping and CNTs. Especially the CNTs can form a multi-channel structure inside the CNF fibers, promoting the conductivities.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further study the intercalation/deintercalation processes, ex-situ Raman spectroscopy was performed at different cycling stages for the CNT/SNCF electrode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a shows that the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio declines from 1.21 to 1.14 during first sodiation, and then returns to 1.24 during the desodiation process. The same change repeats in the next half cycle. The Raman spectra for the potassium storage in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b shows the similar evolution, indicating that the sodium/potassium process is reversible. In addition, the change of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> is usually thought to be related to the change of the degree of graphitization. The above results thus indicate that the intercalation of sodium/potassium can increase the degree of graphitization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref6" aria-label="reference 2 and 6">(2,6)</a> In the literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> it is generally believed that the insertion of sodium/potassium can increase the steric hindrance and stretch the in-plane C-C bonding. However, different observations on the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> have been found. Liu <i>et al</i> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> found that the decrease of graphitization during the potassiation process, while Yu <i>et al</i> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> reported the opposite way. It is assumed that the different behaviour may be caused by the various carbon states which strongly affected by the defects and heteroatoms. The CNT/SNCF electrode processes large lattice distance and high defects, which may contribute to the graphitization during intercalation.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/medium/am1c12470_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a,b) Ex-situ Raman spectra of SIBs (a) and PIBs (b); (c-i) Ex-situ HRTEM of PIBs (after terminal potassiation to 0.01 V at first cycle (0.1 A/g) (c); after terminal depotassiation to 3.0 V at first cycle (0.1 A/g) (d); after terminal depotassiation to 3.0 V at 100 cycle (e); after terminal depotassiation to 3.0 V at 200 cycle (f); after terminal depotassiation to 3.0 V at 500 cycle (g) and after terminal depotassiation to 3.0 V at 1000 cycle (h,i)).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/asset/images/large/am1c12470_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c12470&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The ex-situ HRTEM was also performed to measure the evolution of lattice distance at the specific potassium intercalation/deintecalation stages for the CNT/SNCF. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c, when the potassium is intercalated in the CNT/SNCF electrode to 0.01 V at the first cycle, the interlayer spacings of SNCF (0.43 nm) and CNT (0.40 nm) are appreciably increased compared with the original states in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d<b>&amp;f</b>. The TEM mapping in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a> shows that the potassium is evenly distributed. After depotassiation to 3 V, their interlayer spacings in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d are reduced, close to the original states. For the cycled CNT/SNCF electrodes after 100, 200, 500 and 1000 times, the TEM images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e-i show that the morphology of nanofibers and the interlayer spaces of SNCF and CNT have negligible change, confirming the integrity and high reversibility of the structure due to the enlarged lattice spacing.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have successfully synthesized a free-standing flexible electrode material (CNT/SNCF), and realize the high reversible capacity and long cycle life of sodium and potassium ion storage. Their outstanding storage capacity is due to following points: i) The unique multi-channel 1D/1D structure not only facilitates ion diffusion, then further increases the conductivity and active sites of the material, but also has good flexibility; ii) S and N co-doping can adjust electrical conductivity and improve electrochemical performance; iii) The increased interlayer spacing can effectively alleviate volume changes during cycling, which is beneficial to improve structural stability of the electrode material. Benefited from the above advantages, the CNT/SNCF electrode delivers high discharge capacities of 274.1 and 212.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A/g after 1000 cycles for SIBs and PIBs, respectively. Furthermore, even in high-current cycles, the CNT/SNCF still maintains its morphology without significant loss of holding capacity, indicating that the material has extraordinary structural stability and cycling stability. This work provides a comprehensive view from the morphological and lattice structures in designing and fabricating the flexible electrodes for energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c12470?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c12470</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM, TEM and TEM mapping images; XRD patterns; stress–strain curve; XPS spectra; comparison of different N types and contents; CV curves; charge–discharge curves; Log (i) vs log (<i>v</i>) plots; digital photograph; EIS curves; diffraction peak and FWHM table; and table comparing the performance with literature reports. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c12470/suppl_file/am1c12470_si_001.pdf">am1c12470_si_001.pdf (2.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c12470" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Freestanding film made by necklace-like N-doped hollow carbon with hierarchical pores for high-performance potassium-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Wenxiu; Zhou, Jinhui; Wang, Shuo; Zhang, Weiyu; Wang, Zichen; Lv, Fan; Wang, Kai; Sun, Qiang; Guo, Shaojun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1605-1612</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) have drawn much attention as a replacement for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) owing to their low cost and high safety.  However, it is still an open challenge to design stable and high-capacity nanocarbon for efficient intercalation/deintercalation of large K ions.  Herein, we report a class of ultra-high pyrrolic/pyridinic-N-doped necklace-like hollow carbon (NHC) material as a novel free-standing anode for enhancing PIBs in terms of their capacity, rate ability and durability.  The as-made NHC film features abundant hierarchical micro/meso/macro-pores, a necklace-like hollow structure, ultra-high pyrrolic/pyridinic-N doping and a high sp. surface area, which could finally promote the intercalation/deintercalation of K ions, reduce the vol. expansion and improve the stability of PIBs.  These new characteristics allow the NHC to deliver a high reversible specific capacity of 293.5 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1, outstanding rate property (204.8 mA h g-1 at 2000 mA g-1) and cycling performance (161.3 mA h g-1 at 1000 mA g-1 after 1600 cycles), which represent the best performance for carbon-based non-metal materials for PIB anode.  D. functional theory (DFT) calcns. demonstrate that pyrrolic and pyridinic-N doping can efficiently change the charge d. distribution of carbon and promote the adsorption of K+ on the NHC electrode, which promotes K ion storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpYRSpUBNbPYLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgdbNIuzhzLpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXksVaisbo%253D&amp;md5=c66101903b6051e3ea76fd68e362a185</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201400012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mahmood, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Nanostructures in Lithium-Based Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode Nanostructures in Lithium-Based Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mahmood Nasir; Hou Yanglong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1400012</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-based batteries possessing energy densities much higher than those of the conventional batteries belong to the most promising class of future energy devices.  However, there are some fundamental issues related to their electrodes which are big roadblocks in their applications to electric vehicles (EVs).  Nanochemistry has advantageous roles to overcome these problems by defining new nanostructures of electrode materials.  This review article will highlight the challenges associated with these chemistries both to bring high performance and longevity upon considering the working principles of the various types of lithium-based (Li-ion, Li-air and Li-S) batteries.  Further, the review discusses the advantages and challenges of nanomaterials in nanostructured electrodes of lithium-based batteries, concerns with lithium metal anode and the recent advancement in electrode nanostructures.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRaeSiDnnV1O9NTrvPZP1IpfW6udTcc2eb5D2PuYv9pgrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1c%252FltV2ltQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=edd37c1845c14c7c9ecaf979018e26f0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800171"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sulfur/Oxygen Codoped Porous Hard Carbon Microspheres for High-Performance Potassium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>32</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1904320</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201904320</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D32%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D1904320%26journal%3DAdv.%2BMater.%26author%3DH.%2BHuang%26author%3DR.%2BXu%26author%3DY.%2BFeng%26author%3DS.%2BZeng%26author%3DY.%2BJiang%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DW.%2BLuo%26author%3DY.%2BYu%26title%3DSodium%252FPotassium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BBoosting%2Bthe%2BRate%2BCapability%2Band%2BCycle%2BLife%2Bby%2BCombining%2BMorphology%252C%2BDefect%2Band%2BStructure%2BEngineering%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadma.201904320&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c12470&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadma.201904320&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium/Potassium-Ion Batteries: Boosting the Rate Capability and Cycle Life by Combining Morphology, Defect and Structure Engineering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Huang, Huijuan; Xu, Rui; Feng, Yuezhan; Zeng, Sifan; Jiang, Yu; Wang, Huijuan; Luo, Wei; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1904320</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-based materials have been considered as the most promising anode materials for both sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs), owing to their good chem. stability, high elec. cond., and environmental benignity.  However, due to the large sizes of sodium and potassium ions, it is a great challenge to realize a carbon anode with high reversible capacity, long cycle life, and high rate capability.  Herein, by rational design, N-doped 3D mesoporous carbon nanosheets (N-CNS) are successfully synthesized, which can realize unprecedented electrochem. performance for both SIBs and PIBs.  The N-CNS possess an ultrathin nanosheet structure with hierarchical pores, ultrahigh level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N, and an expanded interlayer distance.  The beneficial features that can enhance the Na-/K-ion intercalation/deintercalation kinetic process, shorten the diffusion length for both ions and electrons, and accommodate the vol. change are demonstrated.  Hence, the N-CNS-based electrode delivers a high capacity of 239 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 10 000 cycles for SIBs and 321 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1 after 5000 cycles for PIBs.  First-principles calcn. shows that the ultrahigh doping level of pyridinic N/pyrrolic N contributes to the enhanced sodium and potassium storage performance by modulating the charge d. distribution on the carbon surface.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVG-cQvHto_LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgdbNIuzhzLpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXpt12kuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=03d6ec4c0f67558dd6c91962115a7d5f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA03939E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kaneti, Y. V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tanaka, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hossain, M. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">15356</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">15366</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C7TA03939E</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D15356-15366%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DY.%2BV.%2BKaneti%26author%3DJ.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.-B.%2BHe%26author%3DZ.%2BWang%26author%3DS.%2BTanaka%26author%3DM.%2BS.%2BA.%2BHossain%26author%3DZ.-Z.%2BPan%26author%3DB.%2BXiang%26author%3DQ.-H.%2BYang%26author%3DY.%2BYamauchi%26title%3DFabrication%2Bof%2BAn%2BMOF-Derived%2BHeteroatom-Doped%2BCo%252FCoO%252FCarbon%2BHybrid%2Bwith%2BSuperior%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC7TA03939E&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c12470&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC7TA03939E&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of an MOF-derived heteroatom-doped Co/CoO/carbon hybrid with superior sodium storage performance for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kaneti, Yusuf Valentino; Zhang, Jun; He, Yan-Bing; Wang, Zhijie; Tanaka, Shunsuke; Hossain, Md. Shahriar A.; Pan, Zheng-Ze; Xiang, Bin; Yang, Quan-Hong; Yamauchi, Yusuke</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">29</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15356-15366</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) have gained significant attention as precursors for the fabrication of porous hybrid materials due to their highly controllable compn., structure and pore size.  However, at present, MOF-derived materials have rarely been studied as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The authors report the fabrication of a Ni-doped Co/CoO/N-doped C (NC) hybrid using bimetallic Ni-Co-ZIF as the starting precursor.  The resulting Ni-doped Co/CoO/NC hybrid is highly microporous with a high sp. surface area of 552 m2 g-1.  When employed as an anode material for Na-ion batteries, the Ni-doped Co/CoO/NC hybrid exhibited both good rate performance with a high discharge capacity of 218 mA h g-1 at a high c.d. of 500 mA g-1 and good cycling stability, as a high discharge capacity of 218.7 mA h g-1 can be retained after 100 cycles at 500 mA g-1, corresponding to a high capacity retention of 87.5%.  The excellent electrochem. performance of the Ni-doped Co/CoO/NC hybrid for SIBs may be attributed to the synergistic effects of various factors, including: (a) the presence of a C matrix which provides protection against aggregation and pulverization during sodiation/desodiation; (b) the highly microporous nature along with the presence of a few mesopores which facilitates better insertion/de-insertion of Na+ ions; (c) the Ni-doping which introduces defect sites into the at. structure of CoO via partial substitution, thus enhancing the cond. of the Co oxide (CoO) component and hence, the overall hybrid material, and (d) the N-doping which promotes a faster migration speed of Na ions (Na+) across the C layer by creating defect sites, thereby improving the cond. of the C frameworks in the hybrid material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqWR9Qrpy6E07Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgdbNIuzhzLpA"> &gt;&gt;
